Qada — Judiciary
beginner
6 min
Right to Revoke Arbitrator Before Decision
States that each party may withdraw from arbitration before a verdict is given

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on revocation of arbitration before a verdict and explain the rationale based on the consensual nature of the appointment.

Core Idea
Arbitration in Hanafi law is consensual — each party may revoke the arbitrator's authority before a decision is pronounced.

This lesson examines a fundamental rule in Hanafi arbitration law: the right of the parties to revoke the arbitrator's authority at any time before a verdict is given. Unlike a state-appointed judge, an arbitrator derives his power solely from the agreement of the disputants. Understanding this principle is essential to grasp the Hanafi perspective on the nature of arbitration and its distinction from formal judicial proceedings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the revocation (ruju') of the arbitrator's authority by either appointing party. The ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) and effective before the arbitrator pronounces a judgment.
The passage uses the phrase 'malam yahkum 'alayhima' (as long as he has not given a judgment against them) to define the temporal limit.
2
Note the legal basis
The arbitrator is not a state judge but a mutually agreed referee. Therefore, his authority is conditional on the continuing consent of both parties. Once consent is withdrawn, his authority ends.
This contrasts with a qadi appointed by the ruler, whose authority is permanent and non-revocable by the litigants.
3
Determine the consequence of revocation
If a party revokes before the decision, any subsequent ruling by the arbitrator is not enforceable. The arbitration ceases to have legal effect.
The passage explicitly states: 'if they take it back, the arbitration will not stand.'
4
Contrast with post-decision revocation
Once the arbitrator pronounces a binding judgment, the right to revoke is lost. The decision becomes binding on both parties.
This temporal limit is critical; after the verdict, the arbitrator's role is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۹۲۵] (۳۸) ولكل واحد من المُحكّمين ان يرجع مالم يحكم عليهما.

حکم بنانے والوں میں سے ہر ایک کے لئے جائز ہے کہ وہ رجوع کریں جب تک کہ ان پر فیصلہ نہ کیا ہو۔ جن لوگوں نے حکم بنایا تھا ان کو یہ اختیار ہے کہ جب تک پہنچ نے کوئی فیصلہ نہیں کیا ہے اس سے پہلے پہلے حکم بنانا واپس لے لیں ۔ اگر انہوں نے واپس لے لیا تو یہ حکم برقرار نہیں رہے گا۔ اور نہ اب اس کا فیصلہ نافذ ہوگا۔ دونوں کے حکم بنانے سے حکم بن تھا اس لئے فیصلہ سے پہلے کم کا انکار کر دے تو وہ انکار کر سکتے ہیں۔ یہ امیر المومنین کی جانب سے حکم نہیں تھا کہ ہمیشہ رہے۔
And each of the two appointers may withdraw as long as he (the arbitrator) has not given a judgment against them. It is permissible for each of those who made the arbitrator to revoke it as long as a decision has not been rendered. The people who appointed the arbitrator have the right to take back the appointment before the arbitrator gives any decision. If they take it back, the arbitration will not stand, and his decision will not be enforced. The arbitration existed because both appointed him, so before the decision, if one of them refuses, he can refuse. This was not a judge appointed by the Commander of the Faithful that would remain permanently.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage gives the rule that the parties who appointed an arbitrator have the right to revoke his authority at any time before he renders a decision. The commentary explains that since the arbitrator is not a state-appointed judge with permanent jurisdiction, his power is derived solely from the consent of the parties. Therefore, if one or both parties withdraw before a verdict, his authority ends and any decision he might give would not be enforceable.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation or withdrawal of the authority given to the arbitrator by one or both parties.
محكمون (muhakkamun): The parties who appoint the arbitrator (the litigants).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Until what point can the parties revoke the arbitrator's authority?
A) At any time, even after the decision
B) Only before the arbitrator gives a judgment
C) Within one week of appointment
D) Only if both parties agree to revoke

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the authority of an arbitrator is derived from the parties' consent and can be revoked by either party before a verdict is rendered.

Qualifications of an Arbitrator and Revocability of Appointment
Ruling on disqualification of arbitrators and revocability of appointment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of individuals who cannot be appointed as arbitrators in Hanafi fiqh and explain the ruling on the validity of their judgment if appointed.

Core Idea
A fasiq's arbitration is enforceable despite prohibition; parties can withdraw before judgment.

This passage from 'Kitab Adab al-Qadi' addresses two key issues: who can be appointed as an arbitrator (hakam) and the revocability of the appointment. It lists several categories of people who are disqualified, yet provides an important practical ruling that if such a person is appointed and issues a judgment, that judgment is enforceable. Additionally, the passage clarifies that the parties may revoke the arbitrator's authority at any time before a decision is rendered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited appointments
The passage lists five categories: a fasiq (sinner), a child (sabi), a slave (abd), one punished for qadhf (mahdud fi al-qadhf), and a non-Muslim (dhimm). Find each and note the ruling: it is not permissible to appoint them as arbitrators.
The matn explicitly mentions fasiq and child; commentary adds others.
2
Examine the proofs (adilla)
For each disqualification, the commentary provides an athar or Quranic verse. For example, for the slave, it cites the statement of several Salaf: 'La tajuza shahadat al-abeed' (The testimony of slaves is not permissible). For the one punished for qadhf, it cites Quran 24:4. For the child, an athar from Ibn Abbas. Identify these proofs and their source.
The proofs are based on analogy from testimony to arbitration.
3
Trace the legal reasoning (wajh al-dalalah)
The reasoning is that arbitration requires credibility and authority similar to testimony. If a person's testimony is not accepted, then his arbitration is also not accepted. For the slave, the argument is that he lacks authority over himself, so he cannot have authority over others. For the child, lack of intellect.
This is a qiyas (analogy) from testimony to arbitration.
4
Note the practical exception for the fasiq
Despite the prohibition, if a fasiq is appointed and gives a judgment, that judgment is enforceable. The commentary gives the example of Hajjaj ibn Yusuf. This illustrates the Hanafi principle of maintaining order (sadd al-dhara'i').
This is an important nuance: the sinfulness of the arbitrator does not automatically invalidate the judgment.
5
Understand the right of withdrawal
The matn states that each party may withdraw the arbitrator's authority as long as no judgment has been passed. This is because the appointment is a delegation (tawkil) from the parties, not a permanent appointment from the ruler. Once judgment is rendered, it is binding and cannot be rescinded.
This reflects the consensual nature of arbitration.

Source Text

كتاب آداب القاضي
والفاسق والصبي [۲۹۲۵] (۳۸) ولكل واحد من المُحكّمين ان يرجع مالم يحكم عليهما.
ص ۴۹۵، نمبر ۲۲۴۳۹ مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب شہادۃ اہل الکفر علی اہل الاسلام، ج ثامن، ص ۱۳۶۰، نمبر ۱۵۵۳۸) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ گواہی بھی جائز نہیں ہے۔ ذمی بھی اسی میں داخل ہے کہ اس کو حکم بنانا بھی جائز نہیں۔ غلام کو حکم بنانا اس لئے صحیح نہیں ہے کہ اس کو اپنے اوپر اختیار نہیں ہے تو دوسرے پر فیصلے کا اختیار کیسے ملے گا (۲) اس کو تو گواہی دینے کی بھی اجازت نہیں ہے فیصلہ کیسے کرے گا۔ اثر میں ہے۔ روی عن على والحسن والنخعى والزهري ومجاهد وعطاء لا تجوز شهادة العبيد (الف) (سن اليقي ، باب من رو شهادة العبيد ومن قبلها ، ج عاشر ص۲۷۲، نمبر ۲۰۶۰۸) اس اثر میں ہے کہ غلام کی گواہی درست نہیں۔ اس لئے اس کو حکم بنانا بھی درست نہیں ہے۔ محدود في القذف کو بھی حکم بنانا درست نہیں ہے۔
آیت میں ہے۔ ولا تقبلوا لهم شهادة ابدا واولئك هم الفاسقون (ب) ( آیت ۴ ، سورۃ النور ۲۴) اس کی جب گواہی مقبول نہیں تو یہ گواہ سے گواہی لیکر فیصلہ کیسے کرے گا۔
فاسق کو حکم بنانا بھی اچھا نہیں ہے کیونکہ وہ عادل نہیں ہے۔ تاہم اگر فاسق کو حکم بنا دیا تو اس کا فیصلہ نافذ ہو جائے گا۔ حجاج بن یوسف فاسق تھا پھر بھی وہ حکم تھا اور اس کے فیصلے نافذ ہوتے تھے۔ اس لئے فاسق کو قاضی یا حکم بنادیا اور اس نے فیصلہ کر دیا تو
نافذ ہو جائے گا۔
بچے اور مجنون کو تو عقل ہی نہیں ہے ان کو حکم کیسے بنائے گا۔ اس کی تو گواہی بھی مقبول نہیں ہے۔ اثر میں ہے۔ ارسل الى ابن عباس . يسأله عن شهادة الصبيان فقال: لا ارى ان تجوز شهادتهم (ج) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب شهادة الصبیان، ج ثامن ، ص ۱۳۴۲۸ نمبر ۱۵۴۹۴) اس اثر میں ہے کہ بچے کی گواہی مقبول نہیں تو اس کو حکم بنانا کیسے درست ہوگا۔
[۲۹۲۵] (۳۸) حکم بنانے والوں میں سے ہر ایک کے لئے جائز ہے کہ وہ رجوع کریں جب تک کہ ان پر فیصلہ نہ کیا ہو۔ جن لوگوں نے حکم بنایا تھا ان کو یہ اختیار ہے کہ جب تک پہنچ نے کوئی فیصلہ نہیں کیا ہے اس سے پہلے پہلے حکم بنانا واپس لے لیں ۔ اگر انہوں نے واپس لے لیا تو یہ حکم برقرار نہیں رہے گا۔ اور نہ اب اس کا فیصلہ نافذ ہوگا۔ دونوں کے حکم بنانے سے حکم بن تھا اس لئے فیصلہ سے پہلے کم کا انکار کر دے تو وہ انکار کر سکتے ہیں۔ یہ امیر المومنین کی جانب سے حکم نہیں تھا
کہ ہمیشہ رہے۔ حاشیہ : (پچھلے صفحہ سے آگے ) نصرانی کی گواہی جائز نہیں ہے مگر سفر میں اور نہیں جائز ہے مگر وصیت میں (الف) حضرت علی حسن بختی، زہری، مجاہد، اور عطاء نے فرمایا کہ غلام کی گواہی جائز نہیں ہے (ب) حد قذف والے کی گواہی کبھی قبول نہ کرو، وہ فاسق ہیں (ج) حضرت ابن عباس کو بچوں کی گواہی کے بارے میں پوچھا فرمایا ان کی گواہی جائز نہیں سمجھتا۔
Book of the Etiquette of the Judge. And the fasiq (sinner) and the child: (38) And it is permissible for each of the two parties who appointed an arbitrator to withdraw as long as the arbitrator has not passed judgment upon them. (Commentary: ... )
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn followed by an expansion. The matn states two things: (1) a fasiq and a child cannot be arbitrators, and (2) each party may withdraw the arbitrator's authority before judgment. The commentary explains the reasons: a fasiq is not just, a child lacks reason, a slave has no authority over himself, and one punished for qadhf (false accusation) has tainted testimony. The commentary cites athar (precedents) from Ali, Hasan, Nakha'i, Zuhri, Mujahid, and Ata that the testimony of a slave is not accepted, and similarly from Ibn Abbas regarding children. The commentary then illustrates that despite the prohibition, if a fasiq is appointed and judges, his judgment is enforced (like Hajjaj ibn Yusuf). The second part of the ruling explains that the appointment is a delegation from the parties, so they may revoke it before execution; once judgment is passed, it cannot be withdrawn. The footnote adds that testimony of a Christian is only allowed in travel and in wills.

Key Terms
حكم (hakam): An arbitrator appointed by disputing parties to settle their dispute, as opposed to a government-appointed judge (qadi).
فاسق (fasiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking moral integrity (adalah).
محدود في القذف (mahrud fi al-qadhf): A person who has received the legal punishment (hadd) for falsely accusing someone of adultery (qadhf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible because children have pure intentions.
B) It is not permissible because a child lacks reason and his testimony is also not accepted.
C) It is permissible only if the child is mature (baligh).
D) It is permissible but the judgment is not binding.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, while appointing a disqualified arbitrator is prohibited, their judgment is enforced to maintain order; parties may withdraw before judgment.

Binding Effect of Arbitrator's Judgment
States the binding effect of arbitrator's judgment with proof from the incident of Bani Qurayza and a mursal hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an arbitrator's judgment is binding on both parties and identify the dalil from the incident of Bani Qurayza and the supporting mursal hadith.

Core Idea
An arbitrator's judgment binds both parties immediately; they cannot revoke consent after the ruling.

This passage establishes a foundational principle of Islamic judicial procedure: the binding nature of arbitration (tahkim). When parties voluntarily submit to an arbitrator, their delegation of authority is irrevocable once the judgment is passed. The author supports this with a key historical precedent from the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and a secondary mursal hadith. Understanding this ruling is essential for anyone involved in dispute resolution, as it clarifies the finality of arbitration decisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the issuance of a judgment by an arbitrator after both parties have submitted to his authority. The ruling is that it becomes binding (wajib) on them.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The author cites the well-known hadith of Bani Qurayza, where the tribe accepted Sa'd ibn Mu'adh as arbitrator and implemented his judgment without objection.
This hadith is narrated by Aisha (ra) in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted Bani Qurayza to choose their own arbitrator and then enforced Sa'd's ruling. This proves that the judgment of a chosen arbitrator is binding; otherwise, the parties could have rejected it.
4
Identify the supporting dalil
A mursal hadith from Hasan al-Basri states that whoever is called to the judgment of an arbitrator and refuses is a wrongdoer (zalim). This reinforces the binding nature.
In Hanafi usul, mursal hadith is accepted as supporting evidence under certain conditions.
5
Determine the authority weight
The primary hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear and explicit (qati al-dalala). The position is from the zahir al-riwayah and is relied upon for fatwa.
There is no intra-Hanafi or inter-madhhab dispute on this fundamental point.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The matn states that once the arbitrator issues a judgment, it is binding on both parties. The commentary explains that this is because the parties voluntarily delegated authority to the arbitrator. The proof is the well-known incident of Bani Qurayza during the battle of the Trench, where the Jewish tribe agreed to accept the judgment of Sa'd ibn Mu'adh (may Allah be pleased with him). When Sa'd ruled that their fighters be killed and women and children taken captive, they complied. This shows that the judgment of a chosen arbitrator binds the parties, as confirmed by the hadith narrated by Aisha. Additionally, a mursal hadith from Hasan al-Basri states that whoever is called to the judgment of an arbitrator and refuses is a wrongdoer (zalim). Therefore, once the judgment is passed, refusal is oppression.

Key Terms
الزم (alzama): To make binding or obligatory; here it means the judgment becomes incumbent upon the parties.
مرسل (mursal): A hadith where the Companion is omitted from the chain; a mursal hadith is considered weaker but can be used as supporting evidence in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Takeaway

Enforcement of Arbitrator's Judgment by a Judge
Rules on judge's duty regarding arbitrator's verdict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding when a judge must enforce or void an arbitrator's judgment, and apply the proof from Sufyan al-Thawri.

Core Idea
An arbitrator's judgment is not final; the judge enforces only if it aligns with his madhhab and Sharia.

In the Hanafi judicial system, an arbitrator (hakam) is not a fully empowered judge. His decisions are subject to review by the formal qadi. This passage explains the qadi's duty: to enforce judgments that conform to his school and Sharia, and to void those that do not. The proof comes from the early authority Sufyan al-Thawri.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the judge's handling of the arbitrator's judgment. The ruling is wajib (obligatory) to enforce if conditions are met, or to void if not.
The obligation is conditional on the judgment's conformity.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the statement of Sufyan al-Thawri, a qawl sahabi/tabi'i, recorded in athar collections.
The dalil is a primary source from early Islamic authority.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Thawri's statement distinguishes between judgments contradicting Quran/Sunnah/Ijma (must be rejected) and those based on valid opinion (should be left). This supports the judge's authority to review and either enforce or void.
The key is whether the judgment violates explicit Sharia sources.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The passage implies that some schools may give less discretion to the judge; the Hanafi position holds that the judge has full discretion based on his madhhab.
Cross-madhhab disagreement exists but the Hanafi position is clear.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling: the judge enforces if the arbitrator's judgment agrees with his madhhab and does not contradict Sharia; otherwise he voids it.
The judge may substitute his own judgment if he voids.

کرے۔ اور اس کو اپنے محور پر رہنے دے۔
40. And when his judgment is raised to the judge, if it is in agreement with the judge's madhhab, he enforces it; and if it contradicts it, he voids it. Commentary: The arbitrator's judgment is brought to the sitting judge. If the judgment agrees with the judge's madhhab and discretion, he enforces it. If it opposes his discretion or opposes Sharia, he rejects it and imposes his own judgment. Since the arbitrator is not a formal judge, his judgment lacks force, so it is at the judge's discretion. However, if it agrees with his madhhab, there is no benefit in breaking it, so he enforces it. Evidence: From al-Thawri: 'If a judge judges contrary to the Book of Allah, the Sunnah of His Prophet, or something on which there is consensus, then the judge after him rejects it. But if it is something based on the opinion of people and not contrary to Sharia, he does not reject it and leaves it in place.' And another: 'I heard Shuraih say...'
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The matn states that when an arbitrator's decision is presented to a formal judge (qadi), the judge must examine it. If the decision conforms to the judge's own school (madhhab) and is in accordance with Sharia, he enforces it. If it contradicts his school or is against the clear texts of Quran, Sunnah, or consensus, he voids it and may substitute his own ruling. The commentary explains that an arbitrator is not a fully empowered judge (qadi bil-rasm), so his judgment does not have absolute authority; the qadi retains supervisory power. The proof is the statement of Sufyan al-Thawri, which indicates that a subsequent judge may overrule any judgment that violates the primary sources of Sharia, but if the judgment is based on valid juristic opinion (ra'y) and does not contradict Sharia, it should be left in place. Another narration from Shuraih (though incomplete here) supports the same. Thus, the arbitrator's judgment is subject to review and possible nullification by the sitting judge.

Key Terms
مذهب (madhhab): The legal school of thought of the judge, specifically the Hanafi madhhab in this context.
امضى (amda): To enforce or execute a judgment; here it means to give effect to the arbitrator's decision.
ابطله (abtala): To void or nullify a judgment; the judge declares the arbitrator's ruling invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only if the arbitrator is appointed by the judge himself.
B) If the judgment agrees with the judge's madhhab and does not contradict Sharia.
C) Always, because the arbitrator's judgment is final.
D) If the arbitrator is a Hanafi scholar.

Key Takeaway
An arbitrator's judgment is subject to review by the judge, who enforces it only if it conforms to his own school and Sharia, based on the authority of Sufyan al-Thawri.

Binding Effect of an Arbitration Ruling in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling that arbitration decision is binding on both parties with hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an arbitrator's decision is binding on both parties and support it with the hadith proof of Sa'd ibn Mu'adh.

Core Idea
Once an arbitrator rules, both parties must comply; the ruling is binding (lazim).

In Hanafi fiqh, arbitration (tahkim) is a valid means of dispute resolution. The key question is whether the arbitrator's decision is binding or merely advisory. This lesson examines the ruling from the classical texts, supported by the famous hadith of Sa'd ibn Mu'adh and a mursal hadith. The conclusion is clear: the ruling is enforceable and must be accepted by both parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is arbitration (tahkim) and the ruling is that the arbitrator's decision (hukm) is binding on both parties (lazim).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith of Sa'd ibn Mu'adh (Bukhari, Muslim). Also a mursal hadith from Bayhaqi.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In the Sa'd hadith, the Banu Qurayzah agreed to accept his judgment, and the Prophet enforced it. This demonstrates binding force. The mursal hadith states that rejecting a valid judgment makes one zalim.
4
Note the Hanafi position
Hanafis consider it binding; some schools may require formal qadi appointment, but the Hanafi view is that consent of parties makes it enforceable.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, once an arbitrator rules, parties cannot reject it arbitrarily; the ruling is final unless there is a clear violation of Shariah.

Source Text

And if he arbitrates between them, it is binding upon them.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that an arbitrator's decision, once issued, is binding on both parties. The commentator provides proof from the hadith of Sa'd ibn Mu'adh, where the Banu Qurayzah agreed to his judgment and it was enforced. Additionally, a marfu' hadith (though mursal) indicates that refusing to accept a valid judgment makes one a wrongdoer (zalim). The footnotes elaborate the full context of the hadith and also include an important principle from Sufyan al-Thawri: if a qadi rules contrary to the Book, Sunnah, or ijma', the subsequent qadi must overturn it; otherwise, if it is a matter of ijtihad, it is left intact. This supports the binding nature of a valid arbitration ruling.

Key Terms
حكم (hakam): An arbitrator chosen by the parties to resolve their dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is binding on both parties and must be accepted.
D) It is binding only in financial matters.

Key Takeaway
An arbitration ruling is binding (lazim) on both parties, established by the practice of the Prophet and the principle that consent to arbitration entails acceptance of its outcome.

8 min
Review of Arbitration Ruling by Qadi: Conformity with Madhhab
Ruling that qadi enforces arbitration if consistent with his madhhab, otherwise voids it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a qadi reviewing an arbitration ruling and apply the rule of conformity with the madhhab to determine whether to enforce or void the ruling.

Core Idea
A qadi enforces an arbitration ruling only if it aligns with his madhhab; otherwise, he voids it.

This lesson examines the authority of a qadi to review arbitration decisions. In Hanafi fiqh, the qadi is not bound by the arbitrator's ruling if it contradicts his own madhhab or clear Shari'a sources. The passage from the text explains the process and provides a proof from the athar of Sufyan al-Thawri and Shuraih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is the qadi doing? Reviewing an arbitration ruling. The ruling: if it matches his madhhab, he enforces it; if it contradicts, he voids it.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is from an athar of Sufyan al-Thawri and Shuraih, which distinguishes between rulings that contradict clear texts (rejected) and those based on ijtihad (accepted).
The athar is a qawl sahabi, considered zanni in thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The athar establishes that a qadi should reject a judgment that goes against Qur'an, Sunnah, or ijma', but accept one based on ijtihad. By analogy, an arbitration ruling that contradicts the qadi's madhhab is akin to contradicting clear texts? Actually, the madhhab is a valid ijtihad, so if it contradicts the qadi's madhhab, it is like a contrary ijtihad, which the qadi may reject.
The Hanafi position gives the qadi discretion to reject rulings based on a different madhhab.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools may grant more finality to arbitration, but Hanafis allow review. The mukhtalaf fih status is noted.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate and based on different usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا رفع حكمه الى القاضي فوقف مذهبه امضاه وان خالفه ابطله.
۲۹۲۷) (۴۰) اگر اس کا فیصلہ قاضی کے پاس لایا جائے اور وہ اس کے مذہب کے موافق ہو تو اس کو نافذ کر دے گا اور اس کے مخالف ہو تو باطل کردے۔ نیچ کا فیصلہ قاضی وقت کے پاس لے جایا گیا۔ پس اگر وہ فیصلہ قاضی کے مذہب اور صواب دید کے مطابق ہو تو قاضی اس کو نافذ کر دے۔ اور اگر وہ ان کی صواب دید کے مخالف ہو یا شریعت کے مخالف ہو تو اس کو رد کر دے اور اپنا فیصلہ نافذ کرے۔ چونکہ یہ با ضابطہ قاضی نہیں ہے اس لئے اس کے فیصلے میں وہ قوت نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے قاضی کے اختیار میں ہے۔ البتہ اس کے مذہب کے موافق ہو تو اس کے توڑنے میں کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہے اس لئے اس کو نافذ کر دے۔ اور مذہب کے مخالف ہو تو رد کر دے(۲) اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن الثوري قال اذا قضى القاضى بخلاف کتاب الله او سنة نبى الله او شيء مجتمع عليه، فان القاضي بعده يرده، فان كان شيئا برای الناس لم يرده ويحمل ذلك ما تحمل (ج) اور دوسری اثر میں ہے۔ سمعت شريحا يقول انی لا ارد قضاء كان قبلی (الف) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب هل يرد قضاء القاضی اور جمع عن قضاه ، ج ثامن ، ص ۱۳۰۲، نمبر ۱۵۲۹۸ر ۱۵۲۹۷) اس اثر میں ہے کہ شریعت کے موافق ہو تو نافذ کرے اور مخالف ہو تو رد کرے، اس پر حکم کے فیصلے کو قیاس کیا جائے گا۔
And if his ruling is presented to the qadi, if it is consistent with his madhhab, he will enforce it; if it contradicts, he will void it.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between arbitration and formal judiciary. When an arbitration ruling is brought before a qadi, the qadi may either confirm or reject it. If the ruling aligns with the qadi's madhhab (school of law) and is in accordance with the Shari'a, the qadi enforces it; otherwise, he may overturn it and issue his own verdict. The commentator draws an analogy from the athar of Sufyan al-Thawri and Shuraih: a qadi should not overturn a previous judgment that is based on ijtihad, but must overturn one that clearly violates the Qur'an, Sunnah, or ijma'. This gives the qadi authority to review arbitration decisions.

Key Terms
مذهب (madhhab): The legal school of the qadi; the arbitration ruling must be consistent with it to be enforced.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the qadi do if an arbitration ruling contradicts his madhhab?
A) He must enforce it anyway to respect the arbitrator's decision.
B) He voids it and may issue his own ruling.
C) He sends it back to the arbitrator for revision.
D) He only enforces it if both parties agree.

Key Takeaway
A qadi is not bound by an arbitration ruling that contradicts his madhhab or clear Shari'a sources; he may void it and issue his own verdict.

Disallowed Arbitration in Hudud and Qisas
Ruling that arbitration is not allowed in hudud and qisas with athar proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on arbitration in hudud and qisas and explain the proof from the athar of Sufyan al-Thawri.

Core Idea
Arbitration is void in hudud and qisas — these cases belong only to the appointed qadi.

This lesson addresses a key limitation of arbitration (tahkim) in Hanafi fiqh: it is not permitted for hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retribution). The source presents a clear ruling and an athar from Sufyan al-Thawri as evidence, highlighting that these serious matters require a formally appointed qadi. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying arbitration in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is arbitration (tahkim) in cases of hudud and qisas. The ruling is that it is not permissible (haram).
The text states: 'ولا يجوز التحكيم في الحدود والقصاص'.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is an athar (narration) from Sufyan al-Thawri, recorded in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq. The athar states that arbitration is generally valid except in hudud.
The athar is: 'قال سفيان اذا حكم رجلان حكما فقضى بينهما فقضاء ٥ جائز الا في الحدود'.
3
Examine the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit exception ('الا في الحدود') in the athar indicates that hudud (and by analogy, qisas) are excluded from valid arbitration. The exclusion proves the ruling of impermissibility.
The Hanafi principle: an exception in a general statement indicates that the excepted case has a different ruling.
4
Consider the scope
The ruling only applies to hudud and qisas. In other matters (mu'amalat and other disputes), arbitration is permissible. This is confirmed by the same athar.
The text adds: 'البتہ معاملات میں بنا سکتا ہے' (however, in transactions it can be done).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage prohibits arbitration (tahkim) in cases of hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retribution). The commentator explains that these matters are serious and fall exclusively under the jurisdiction of a formally appointed qadi. The athar from Sufyan al-Thawri confirms that arbitration is valid except in hudud, where it is not allowed. Thus, in all other matters (mu'amalat), arbitration is permissible.

Key Terms
حدود (hudud): Fixed punishments for crimes against God (e.g., theft, zina).
قصاص (qisas): Retributive justice for bodily harm or murder, requiring equal retaliation or compensation.
تحكيم (tahkim): Arbitration; appointment of a private arbitrator to resolve a dispute.

Invalidity of Arbitration in Accidental Killing (Diyat)
Ruling that arbitration in accidental killing is invalid qiyasan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on arbitration in accidental killing and explain the qiyas that extends the prohibition from qisas to qatl khata.

Core Idea
Accidental killing is qisas-like; thus arbitration is as invalid as in hudud.

We have already learned that arbitration (tahkim) is permissible outside the sphere of hudud (fixed punishments). But what about accidental killing (qatl khata), where the penalty is diya (blood money) rather than qisas (retaliation)? The Hanafi school rules that arbitration remains invalid even in such cases. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: accidental killing is legally classified under qisas, and qisas itself is treated like hudud for the purpose of arbitration. Thus, even if the arbitrator awards diya, his ruling carries no effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is appointing an arbitrator (tahkim) in a case of accidental killing (qatl khata). The ruling: the arbitrator’s verdict is invalid (batil) and unenforceable, even if he awards diya.
Note: The ruling applies regardless of the arbitrator’s decision—only a formal qadi can adjudicate such cases.
2
Recall the general principle on arbitration
The base principle (established earlier) is that arbitration is valid in all matters except hudud (and qisas, as a subset of hudud). This is the original case (asl) for the qiyas.
The permissibility of arbitration outside hudud is based on practice and athar (e.g., from Sufyan al-Thawri).
3
Identify the new case (far') and its apparent difference
The new case is accidental killing (qatl khata), which results in diya (financial compensation) rather than qisas (physical retaliation). At first glance, it appears to be a financial matter, which could allow arbitration.
This is the key point of contention: does the financial nature of diya remove it from the prohibition?
4
Determine the effective cause (illa) for the prohibition
The Hanafi analysis finds that accidental killing falls under the category of qisas because it involves the loss of life. Qisas, in turn, is included within hudud by way of similarity (shabah)—both involve rights of Allah and require strict evidentiary standards. Thus, the illa is that qatl khata is a form of qisas, and qisas is like hudud.
The illa is extracted via tahqiq al-manat: the common element is the taking of life, which triggers the special treatment.
5
Apply the illa to derive the ruling
Since the illa (being qisas-like) is present in accidental killing, the prohibition of arbitration applies equally. Hence, the arbitrator's ruling in qatl khata is invalid, just as it would be in intentional qisas or hudud.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) because the similarity is not immediately obvious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان حكما في دم الخطأ فقضى الحاكم على العاقلة بالدية لم ينفذ حكمه
[۲۹۲۹) (۴۲) اگر دونوں نے حکم بنایا قتل خطا کے دم میں، پس حکم نے عاقلہ پر دیت کا فیصلہ کیا تو اس کا حکم نافذ نہیں ہوگا۔ قتل خطا قصاص کے احکامات میں سے ہے۔ اس لئے اس میں قاضی کا فیصلہ چلے گا۔ اس میں حکم نہیں بنانا چاہئے ۔ اس صورت میں اگر چہ دیت یعنی مدعی علیہ پر مال کا فیصلہ کیا ہے، تاہم یہ قصاص کے احکامات میں سے ہے اس لئے حکم کا حکم نافذ نہیں ہوگا۔ او پر اثر گزر چکا کہ حدود کے علاوہ میں حکم بنا سکتا ہے۔ اور قصاص بھی حدود میں داخل ہے۔
And if they appoint an arbitrator in accidental killing, and the arbitrator rules that the 'aqila pay diya, his ruling is not enforceable.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage extends the prohibition of arbitration to cases of accidental killing (qatl khata). Although the punishment is diya (blood money) rather than qisas, the commentator argues that accidental killing falls under the category of qisas, so arbitration is invalid. The reasoning is that qisas is considered a form of hudud, and as established, arbitration is not allowed in hudud. Therefore, even if the arbitrator orders the 'aqila to pay diya, the ruling has no effect; only a formal qadi can deal with such cases.

Key Terms
عاقلة (aqila): The paternal relatives of the killer who collectively pay diyat (blood money) for accidental killing.
حكم (hakam): Arbitrator appointed by both parties to resolve a dispute; his ruling is called tahkim.
قتل خطا (qatl khata): Accidental killing, where there is no intent; the penalty is diya (blood money) and kaffara (expiation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is invalid because accidental killing falls under qisas, which is like hudud.
C) It is valid only if both parties agree to the arbitrator's decision.
D) It is invalid only if the diya exceeds a certain amount.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, arbitration (tahkim) is invalid in accidental killing because it is classified under qisas, and qisas is treated like hudud, where no private arbitration is permitted.

Arbitrator's Authority to Rule Based on Nukul: Qiyas to Qadi
Ruling that arbitrator may hear witnesses and rule based on refusal to swear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the arbitrator's authority to rule on nukul and apply the qiyas analogy from the qadi.

Core Idea
An arbitrator may rule based on the defendant's refusal to take an oath, just as a qadi may.

This passage details the procedural powers of an arbitrator (hakam). It establishes that an arbitrator may hear witnesses and, if the plaintiff has none, ask the defendant to take an oath. If the defendant refuses (nukul), the arbitrator rules in favor of the plaintiff. This mirrors the powers of a qadi, derived through a clear qiyas analogy. Understanding this ruling clarifies the scope of private arbitration in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states that it is permissible (ja'iz) for the arbitrator to hear witnesses (bayanat) and to rule based on nukul (refusal to take an oath).
This is the operative ruling (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school.
2
Recognize the Asl (Original Case)
The basis of the analogy is the qadi in a formal court, who may rule based on witnesses or nukul.
The qadi's authority is derived from general principles of judiciary (qada).
3
Understand the Illa (Effective Cause)
The effective cause connecting the arbitrator to the qadi is that both perform the function of dispute resolution with the consent of the parties.
The illa is jaliyyah (clear) and extracted by tahrir al-manat.
4
Apply Qiyas
Since the arbitrator shares the same illa (dispute resolution function), the same ruling applies: the arbitrator may also rule based on witnesses and nukul.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
5
Note the Thamarah (Practical Consequence)
The practical effect is that parties in private arbitration have the same procedural safeguards as in court: the plaintiff can compel an oath, and the defendant's refusal results in a ruling against them.
This strengthens the legitimacy of arbitration in Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز ان يسمع البينة ويقضى بالنكول
[۲۹۳٠) (۴۳) حکم کے لئے جائز ہے کہ گواہوں کی بات سنے اور قسم کھانے سے انکار سے بھی فیصلہ کرے۔ فیصلہ کرنے کے دو طریقے ہیں۔ ایک تو گواہوں کی گواہی سے اور اس پر فیصلہ کرے۔ دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ مدعی کے پاس گواہ نہیں ہے، اب وہ مدعی علیہ کو تم کھانے کے لئے کہے، وہ قسم کھانے سے انکار کر جائے تو حکم مدعی کے حق میں فیصلہ کر دے۔ جس طرح قاضی کو دونوں اختیار ہیں پنچ کو بھی دونوں طریقوں سے فیصلے کا اختیار ہے۔ اور ایک تیسرا طریقہ یہ ہے کہ مدعی علیہ حق کا اقرار کرے تب بھی فیصلہ کر سکتا ہے، پنچ کو اس کا بھی اختیار ہے۔ اصل یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ پنچ بہت سے معاملات میں قاضی کی طرح ہے۔ النکول : قسم کھانے سے انکار کرنے کو کول کہتے ہیں۔
And it is permissible for the arbitrator to hear witnesses and rule based on nukul (refusal to take an oath).
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage details the procedural powers of an arbitrator (hakam). The arbitrator has the authority to hear witnesses (bayanat) and pass judgment. Additionally, if the plaintiff has no witnesses, the arbitrator may ask the defendant to take an oath (yamin). If the defendant refuses (nukul), the arbitrator rules in favor of the plaintiff. This mirrors the powers of a qadi. The commentator also mentions a third method: if the defendant confesses, the arbitrator can also rule accordingly. The underlying principle is that in many respects, the arbitrator is treated like a qadi.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukul): The defendant's refusal to take an oath after being required to do so; treated as an admission in favor of the plaintiff.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) He can appoint experts to investigate.

D) He can require both parties to pay fees.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that an arbitrator may rule based on the defendant's refusal to take an oath (nukul) by qiyas to a qadi.

Qadi's Judgment for Close Relatives Is Void
Ruling that a qadi's judgment for his own close relatives is void

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a qadi's judgment for his own parents, children, or wife is void, and explain the underlying principle of conflict of interest.

Core Idea
A qadi's ruling for his own close relatives is automatically void—conflict of interest invalidates the judgment.

Imagine a judge ruling in a case where his own parent or child is a party. Can we trust his impartiality? Islamic law firmly says no. This lesson examines the clear Hanafi ruling that such a judgment is null and void, regardless of the judge's personal integrity. We will explore the scope of this rule and note some nuanced positions from early authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is a qadi issuing a judgment that benefits his parents, children, or wife. The ruling is that such a judgment is batil (void).
2
Understand the reason (illah)
Why is it void? The underlying cause is the obvious conflict of interest—the qadi cannot be impartial when adjudicating for his own close relatives. This principle protects the integrity of the judiciary.
The illah is clear and widely accepted.
3
Determine the scope of relatives
Which relatives are included? The text mentions parents, children, and wife. By extension, it includes all ascendants and descendants, as well as the spouse. Other close relations like siblings may also be covered by analogy.
Some later Hanafi texts extend this to any relative who would inherit from the qadi.
4
Consider the consequences
What happens to such a judgment? It is void ab initio—it never had legal effect. The case must be retried before an impartial qadi. The qadi may also be subject to disciplinary action.
5
Analyze the footnotes on differing views
The source includes statements from Shuraih and Sufyan. Shuraih says he would not overturn a previous judgment; Sufyan limits the invalidity to cases of arbitration not involving hudud. These are minority views; the established Hanafi position remains that the judgment is void.
These footnotes show that even within the tradition, there were nuances, but the operative fatwa is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكم الحاكم لابويه وولده وزوجته باطل.
[۲۹۳۱] (۴۴) حاکم کا فیصلہ اپنے والدین کے لئے اور اپنی اولاد کے لئے اور اپنی بیوی کے لئے باطل ہے۔
حاشیه : (الف) حضرت شریح فرماتے ہیں کہ مجھ سے پہلے کے فیصلے کو میں رد نہیں کروں گا (ب) حضرت سفیان نے فرمایا اگر دو فریقوں نے کسی کو حکم بنایا اور انہوں نے ان دونوں کے درمیان کوئی فیصلہ کیا تو اس کا فیصلہ جائز ہے مگر حدود میں حکم کا فیصلہ جائز نہیں ہے۔
And the ruling of the qadi for his parents, his children, and his wife is void.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage states that a qadi's judgment in favor of his own parents, children, or spouse is invalid (batil). The reason is the obvious conflict of interest: the qadi cannot be impartial when his own relatives are involved. The footnote from Shuraih indicates that he would not overturn a previous judgment, but that is a separate principle. The second footnote from Sufyan reiterates the exception of hudud in arbitration, which is consistent with earlier rulings. The passage implies that the qadi should recuse himself in such cases, and if he does not, his ruling is void.

Key Terms
حاكم (hakim): Here it refers to the qadi (judge) himself.
باطل (batil): Void, null, having no legal effect.
حكم (hukm): Judgment, decree, ruling of a qadi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is valid if no other qualified judge is available.
B) It is void (batil) because of the conflict of interest.
C) It is valid only if the child is not a minor.
D) It is valid but can be appealed.

Key Takeaway
A qadi's judgment for his own parents, children, or wife is automatically void in Hanafi law, preserving judicial impartiality and public trust.

Arbitration Prohibited in Hudud and Qisas
Ruling on prohibition of arbitration in hudud and qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on arbitration in hudud and qisas and explain the evidence from the athar.

Core Idea
Arbitration is invalid in hudud and qisas because these rights of Allah require a state judge.

This lesson examines a specific ruling within the qada (judiciary) chapter: the prohibition of private arbitration in hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation). While arbitration is generally valid in commercial and other transactions, the Hanafi school excludes these serious matters from private settlement. The primary evidence is an athar from Sufyan al-Thawri, which explicitly excepts hudud from valid arbitration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is tahkim (arbitration) in cases of hudud and qisas. The ruling is that it is not permissible (la yajuz), meaning invalid or haram.
The term 'la yajuz' in Hanafi fiqh typically indicates prohibition.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites an athar from Sufyan al-Thawri narrated in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq. Identify the nature of the evidence: it is a report from a tabi'i, considered as Sunnah ahad.
This proof has zanni thubut but qati dalala due to explicit wording.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The athar states: 'When two men appoint an arbitrator and he judges between them, his judgment is valid except in hudud.' The exception 'illa fi al-hudud' clearly excludes hudud. By qiyas, qisas is also included because it shares the same characteristic of being a fixed right of Allah.
The Hanafi principle of qiyas extends the ruling from hudud to qisas due to the common illa.
4
Note the cross-madhhab status
Some schools may allow arbitration in these matters, but the Hanafi school maintains the prohibition. The athar is specific to hudud, and the Hanafis extend it to qisas.
This is a point of ikhtilaf among the schools.
5
Understand the rationale
The commentary explains that hudud and qisas are important matters that must be decided by a state judge (qadi) because they involve rights of Allah and public order. Private arbitrators lack the authority and procedural strictness required.
This rationale supports the ruling and is consistent with the Hanafi emphasis on state authority.

Arbitration Invalid in Accidental Killing
Ruling on arbitration in accidental killing case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that arbitration in accidental killing is invalid and explain why the arbitrator's decision is unenforceable.

Core Idea
Even if arbitration results in a financial penalty, it is invalid in all qisas matters, including accidental killing.

In Hanafi fiqh, arbitration (tahkim) is generally permissible except in certain cases. This lesson examines an extension of that exception: accidental killing (qatl khata'). Although the outcome is payment of diya (blood money) by the 'aqila, it is treated as part of qisas and therefore falls under the prohibition. The proof is a prior athar that excludes hudud and qisas from arbitration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being judged? It is arbitration (tahkim) in a case of accidental killing. According to the Hanafi school, this act is haram and the resulting ruling is void and unenforceable.
2
Understand the legal categorization
Accidental killing is categorized under qisas (retaliation) even though the penalty is financial (diya). This is key because the prohibition extends to all qisas matters.
Diya is the compensation, but the case originates from homicide, so it remains in the realm of qisas.
3
Apply the previous athar
A prior established athar (narration) states that arbitration is not allowed in hudud. The Hanafi jurists include qisas under this prohibition because qisas is considered similar to hudud in severity.
The exact text of the athar is not quoted here, but its principle is applied.
4
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, an arbitrator's ruling in an accidental killing case—even if it orders diya from the 'aqila—is invalid and not enforced. The case must go to a state judge.
This extends the prohibition beyond pure hudud offenses to all qisas-related claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۹۲۹) (۴۲) وان حماه في دم الخطأ فقضى الحاكم على العاقلة بالدية لم ينفذ حكمه [۲۹۲۹) (۴۲) اگر دونوں نے حکم بنایا قتل خطا کے دم میں، پس حکم نے عاقلہ پر دیت کا فیصلہ کیا تو اس کا حکم نافذ نہیں ہوگا۔ قتل خطا قصاص کے احکامات میں سے ہے۔ اس لئے اس میں قاضی کا فیصلہ چلے گا۔ اس میں حکم نہیں بنانا چاہئے ۔ اس صورت میں اگر چہ دیت یعنی مدعی علیہ پر مال کا فیصلہ کیا ہے، تاہم یہ قصاص کے احکامات میں سے ہے اس لئے حکم کا حکم نافذ نہیں ہوگا۔ او پر اثر گزر چکا کہ حدود کے علاوہ میں حکم بنا سکتا ہے۔ اور قصاص بھی حدود میں داخل ہے۔

Even though diya (financial compensation) is a property ruling, it is part of the rulings of qisas, so the arbitrator's ruling is not enforced. The previous athar established that arbitration is allowed except in hudud, and qisas is included in hudud.

Commentary
This passage extends the prohibition of arbitration to cases of accidental killing (qatl khata') where the arbitrator rules that the 'aqila (the tribal group responsible for paying diya) must pay blood money. Although the ruling involves property (diya), the case is still categorized under qisas (retaliation) because it originates from killing. Since qisas is included in the prohibition (as per the previous athar that excludes hudud, and qisas is akin to hudud), the arbitrator's ruling is null and void. The correct authority is the state judge. This lesson type is a ruling with proof (the previous athar serves as the basis).

Key Terms
دم الخطأ (dam al-khata): Accidental killing: homicide without intent, requiring diya (blood money) paid by the 'aqila.
عاقلة (aqila): The group of male relatives (tribe) responsible for paying diya on behalf of the killer in accidental killing.

The Arbitrator's Authority: Ruling by Witnesses and Nukul
Permissibility for arbitrator to use witnesses and nukul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three methods an arbitrator may use to decide a case and explain the legal basis for the arbitrator’s authority similar to a judge.

Core Idea
An arbitrator shares a judge's authority to rule based on witnesses, nukul, or confession.

This lesson examines the powers of an arbitrator in Hanafi fiqh. The text states that an arbitrator may hear witnesses or rely on the defendant’s refusal to take an oath (nukul). The commentary expands that the arbitrator also may use confession, and that the arbitrator is like a judge in many matters. We will explore the legal reasoning behind this permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The act is the arbitrator's decision-making process. The ruling is permissibility (ja'iz)—it is allowed for the arbitrator to use these methods.
2
List the permissible methods
The passage mentions two explicit methods: hearing witnesses (bayyinah) and ruling based on nukul (refusal to swear). The commentary adds a third: confession (iqrar) of the defendant.
These three correspond to the three usual bases of judgment in Hanafi law.
3
Understand the legal basis
The commentary states the principle: 'the arbitrator is like a judge in many matters' (al-hakam ka al-qadi fi ba'd al-umur). This analogy extends the judge's authority to the arbitrator in evidentiary procedures.
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) based on the shared function of deciding disputes.
4
Define key terms
Bayyinah: evidence, typically the testimony of witnesses. Nukul: the defendant's refusal to take a sworn oath after being asked by the plaintiff. Iqrar: confession or admission of the claim.
Nukul results in a ruling against the defendant because the refusal implies the truth of the plaintiff's claim.
5
Note any conditions or limitations
The passage does not mention specific conditions for the arbitrator's use of these methods. However, the analogy to a judge implies that the arbitrator must follow the rules of evidence applicable to a qadi (e.g., number of witnesses, qualification).
For nukul, the plaintiff must request the oath; the defendant's refusal is then treated as a confession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۹۳٠ (۴۳) ويجوز ان يسمع البيئة ويقضى بالنكول [ ۲۹۳۱ (۴۴) وحكم الحاكم لابويه وولده وزوجته باطل. [۲۹۳۰) (۴۳) حکم کے لئے جائز ہے کہ گواہوں کی بات سنے اور قسم کھانے سے انکار سے بھی فیصلہ کرے۔ فیصلہ کرنے کے دو طریقے ہیں۔ ایک تو گواہوں کی گواہی سے اور اس پر فیصلہ کرے۔ دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ مدعی کے پاس گواہ نہیں ہے، اب وہ مدعی علیہ کو تم کھانے کے لئے کہے، وہ قسم کھانے سے انکار کر جائے تو حکم مدعی کے حق میں فیصلہ کر دے۔ جس طرح قاضی کو دونوں اختیار ہیں پنچ کو بھی دونوں طریقوں سے فیصلے کا اختیار ہے۔ اور ایک تیسرا طریقہ یہ ہے کہ مدعی علیہ حق کا اقرار کرے تب بھی فیصلہ کر سکتا ہے، پنچ کو اس کا بھی اختیار ہے۔ اصل یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ پنچ بہت سے معاملات میں قاضی کی طرح ہے۔ النکول : قسم کھانے سے انکار کرنے کو کول کہتے ہیں۔
(43) And it is permissible for him to hear the witnesses and rule based on nukul (refusal to swear). (43) It is permissible for the arbitrator to hear the testimony of witnesses and also to decide based on the defendant's refusal to take an oath. There are two methods of deciding. One is through the testimony of witnesses and he decides based on that. The second method is that the plaintiff has no witnesses; he asks the defendant to take an oath, and if he refuses, the arbitrator decides in favor of the plaintiff. Just as the judge has both options, the arbitrator also has authority to decide through both methods. And there is a third method: the defendant confesses to the right; then too he can decide. The arbitrator also has this authority. The basis of this issue is the principle that in many matters, the arbitrator is like a judge. 'Nukul' means refusal to take an oath.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage describes the authority of an arbitrator (hakam) to reach a verdict using two main methods: (1) hearing witnesses (bayyinah) and (2) relying on the defendant's refusal to swear an oath (nukul). The commentary explains that the arbitrator has the same powers as a judge in this regard. Additionally, a third method is mentioned: the defendant's confession (iqrar). The basis is that an arbitrator is like a judge in many matters (al-hakam ka al-qadi fi ba'd al-umur). This ruling type is a statement of permissibility with no explicit proof quoted.

Key Terms
نكول (nukul): The defendant's refusal to take a sworn oath after being requested by the plaintiff; it results in a ruling against the defendant.
بيئة (bayyinah): Evidence, specifically testimony of witnesses that establishes a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

An arbitrator in Hanafi law can rule using witnesses, nukul, or confession, by virtue of being akin to a judge.

Judge's Ruling for Close Relatives Is Invalid
Ruling on judge's ruling for close relatives with proofs from athar and qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a judge's decision for close relatives and explain the proof from Umar's athar and the analogy from testimony rules.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling for parents, children, or his wife is batil due to the strong presumption of bias.

This lesson addresses a key principle in Islamic judicial ethics: a judge cannot issue a valid ruling in favor of his closest relatives. Even if the judge is truly impartial, the ruling is deemed void to eliminate suspicion and preserve the integrity of the judiciary. We'll examine the ruling and its proofs from the practice of Umar and rules of testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states that a judge's (qadi or arbitrator) ruling for his parents, children, or wife is batil (invalid). Note that it applies only to rulings in their favor, not against them.
The term حاكم includes both qadi and arbitrator.
2
Analyze the first proof: Umar's athar
Umar did not judge his own case but appointed Zayd ibn Thabit. This shows that even the caliph must avoid the appearance of bias. By qiyas, a judge must avoid judging for close relatives.
The illa is suspicion of bias (تہمت).
3
Analyze the second proof: Testimony analogy
An athar from Ibrahim lists persons whose testimony is not admissible, including parent for child and husband for wife. If testimony is disallowed, then ruling in their favor is even more prohibited.
This is an a fortiori argument (اولویت).
4
Determine the thamarah (practical consequence)
The ruling is batil only if it benefits the relative. A ruling against them is valid. This ensures justice while preventing bias.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ ۲۹۳۱ (۴۴) وحكم الحاكم لابويه وولده وزوجته باطل. [۲۹۳۱] (۴۴) حاکم کا فیصلہ اپنے والدین کے لئے اور اپنی اولاد کے لئے اور اپنی بیوی کے لئے باطل ہے۔ حاکم چاہے قاضی ہو یا پنچ ہو اپنے والدین کے لئے ، اپنی اولاد کے لئے یا اپنی بیوی کے لئے فیصلہ کرے تو وہ باطل ہے۔ البتہ ان کے خلاف فیصلہ کرے تو نافذ ہوگا۔ یہ لوگ قریبی رشتہ دار ہیں اس لئے شبہ ہے کہ ان کی رعایت کر کے فیصلہ کیا ہوگا۔ اس لئے ان کے حق میں فیصلہ باطل ہے (۲) اثر میں ہے کہ حضرت عمر امیر المومنین تھے اس زمانے میں کسی پر ان کا حق تھا تو خود فیصلہ نہیں فرمایا بلکہ حضرت زید بن ثابت کو فیصلے کا حکم بنایا۔ اثر یہ ہے۔ سمعت الشعبي قال: كان بين عمر وابي خصومة فقال عمر اجعل بینی و بینک رجلا قال فجعلا بینهما زید بن ثابت قال فأتوه قال فقال عمر اتيناك لتحكم بيننا الخ (الف) سن للبیهقی ، باب القاضی لا یحکم لنفسه ، ج عاشر ص ۲۴۳ نمبر ۲۰۵۱۰) اس اثر میں ہے کہ قاضی اپنے معاملے کا فیصلہ خود نہ کرے۔ کیونکہ تہمت ہوگی۔ اس پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے قریبی رشتہ دار کا بھی فیصلہ نہ کرے کیونکہ رعایت کرنے کی تہمت ہوگی (۳) ان لوگوں کے لئے گواہی جائز نہیں ہے تو فیصلہ کیسے جائز ہوگا ۔ اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابراهیم قال اربعة لا تجوز شهادتهم الوالد لولده، والولد لوالده، والمرأة لزوجها والزوج لامرأته، والعبد لسيده والسيد لعبده، والشريك لشريكه في الشيء اذا كان بينهما، واما فيما سوى ذلك فشهادته جائزة (ب)(مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب شہادۃ الاخ لاحیہ والا بن لابیہ والزوج لامرائته، ج ثامن ، ص ۱۳۴۴، نمبر ۱۵۴۷۶/ مصنف ابن ابی شيبة ، ۴۲۵ في شهادة والولد لوالده ، ج رابع ، ص ۵۳۲ ، نمبر ۲۲۸۵) اس اثر میں ہے کہ والدین اور بچے اور بیوی کے لئے گواہی جائز نہیں تو ان کے حق میں فیصلہ کیسے جائز ہوگا ؟
(44) And the ruling of the judge for his parents, his child, and his wife is invalid. (44) The ruler's (judge or arbitrator) ruling for his parents, his children, and his wife is invalid. Whether the ruler is a judge or an arbitrator, if he decides for his parents, children, or wife, it is invalid. However, if he decides against them, it is enforceable. These are close relatives, so there is suspicion that he may have favored them. Therefore, a ruling in their favor is invalid. (2) There is an athar that Umar (as Amir al-Mu'minin) had a claim against someone; he did not judge himself but appointed Zayd ibn Thabit as arbitrator. The athar: I heard al-Sha'bi say: There was a dispute between Umar and my father; Umar said: Appoint a man between me and you. So they appointed Zayd ibn Thabit. They came to him and Umar said: We have come to you to judge between us... (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter: The judge should not judge for himself). This athar states that a judge should not judge his own case because of suspicion. By analogy, he should not judge for close relatives because of suspicion of favoritism. (3) Testimony for these people is not permissible; so how can a ruling be permissible? There is an athar from Ibrahim: Four people's testimony is not permissible: a parent for his child, a child for his parent, a woman for her husband, a husband for his wife, a slave for his master, a master for his slave, and a partner for his partner in a shared property. But for others, testimony is permissible.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage states that a judge's (or arbitrator's) ruling in favor of his parents, children, or wife is invalid due to the suspicion of bias. The commentary provides two proofs: (1) an athar from Umar who did not judge his own case but appointed an arbitrator, showing that a judge should avoid even the appearance of bias; by analogy (qiyas), this applies to close relatives. (2) An athar from Ibrahim that testimony between these relatives is not admissible; if testimony is not allowed, then ruling based on that testimony or even a direct ruling in their favor is also invalid. The ruling is only invalid if it is in their favor; a ruling against them is valid. This lesson type is a ruling with proof.

Key Terms
باطل (batil): Invalid; having no legal effect. The ruling is considered void and unenforceable.
حاكم (hakim): Anyone with authority to judge, including a state judge (qadi) or an arbitrator (hakam).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Suspended

Key Takeaway
A judge's ruling in favor of close relatives is void (batil), but against them is valid, due to the presumption of bias.

Division Without Proof of Acquisition
Ruling on dividing jointly owned property without stating how ownership was obtained

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when joint possessors request division without proving acquisition and identify the legal basis (ẓāhirī qarīnah) that justifies it.

Core Idea
When joint possession is established, the qadi may divide property without investigating the origin of ownership.

In this passage, we encounter a pragmatic ruling from Hanafi fiqh regarding judicial division of property. The text addresses a common scenario: several people claim joint ownership of an item and request the qadi to divide it, but they provide no evidence of how they came to own it. The Hanafi position is that the qadi may proceed with division based solely on their physical possession. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is dividing property among joint claimants. The ruling is jāʾiz (permissible) under certain conditions.
2
Determine the condition for this ruling
The key condition is that the claimants are in physical possession (qabḍ) of the property.
Without possession, the ruling does not apply.
3
Understand the legal basis (ẓāhirī qarīnah)
Possession is considered an apparent indication (ẓāhirī qarīnah) of ownership in Hanafi law. This allows the qadi to rely on it.
No further proof is needed because there is no absent party (qadā ʿalā al-ghāʾib).
4
Note what is not required
Testimony or investigation into how ownership occurred is not required. The qadi does not need to call witnesses or examine documents.
This streamlines the process when possession is clear.
5
Apply the ruling to hypothetical scenarios
Consider variations: what if only one party has possession? What if the property is land with unclear boundaries? The ruling holds only when all claimants are in joint possession.
Boundary cases help reinforce the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر کچھ لوگ قاضی کے پاس آکر یہ کہیں کہ یہ چیز ہماری ملکیت ہے اس کو تقسیم کر دیں لیکن یہ نہ بتائے کہ ان لوگوں کی ملکیت کیسے ہوئی ، خریدنے کی وجہ سے یا وراثت کی وجہ سے۔ پھر بھی قاضی کو اختیار ہے کہ اس چیز کو ان کے درمیان تقسیم کر دے۔ جب ان کے قبضے میں ہے تو ظاہری قرینہ یہی ہے کہ ان کی ہی ملکیت ہے اس لئے تقسیم کر سکتا ہے (۲) اس میں قضا علی الغیر نہیں ہے اس لئے گواہی کی اور اس تحقیق کی کہ کس طرح اس کی ملکیت ہوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے اس کو تقسیم کر دے۔
If some people come to the qadi and say, 'This thing is our property, divide it among us,' but they do not state how they acquired ownership, whether by purchase or inheritance, even then the qadi has the authority to divide it among them. Since it is in their possession, the apparent indication is that it is their property, so he can divide it. In this there is no judgment against an absent person, so testimony or investigation into how ownership occurred is not needed. Therefore, he divides it.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on the permissibility of dividing property when the claimants do not specify the mode of acquisition. The passage type is a ruling statement. The author explains that when people come to the qadi claiming joint ownership of a property and they are in physical possession, the qadi may divide it among them even if they do not explain how they came to own it (e.g., by purchase or inheritance). The reasoning is that possession (qabḍ) is an external indication (qarīnah ẓāhiriyyah) of ownership. Since there is no absent party involved (qadā ʿalā al-ghāʾib), the qadi does not need to require testimony or investigate the origin of ownership. The ruling is based on the principle that apparent control supports the claim of ownership. This is a practical fiqh ruling that facilitates division in cases where proof of transfer is unclear but possession is established.

Key Terms
ظاہری قرینہ (ẓāhirī qarīnah): An external indication or circumstantial evidence that is apparent and considered sufficient for legal judgment in this context.
قاضی (qāḍī): A judge in Islamic law who adjudicates disputes.
قبض (qabḍ): Physical possession or control over an item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the parties have already testified

Key Takeaway
When joint possessors request division, the qadi may rely on possession as an apparent indication of ownership and proceed without investigating how ownership was obtained.

Compulsory Division When All Partners Benefit
Ruling on compulsory division when no harm to any partner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for compulsory division in Hanafi fiqh and apply it to a scenario of joint ownership.

Core Idea
If division causes no harm, one partner can compel division.

This lesson examines a key ruling on joint property: when can a single co-owner force a partition? The passage makes it clear that if every partner can gain independent benefit from his share and no one suffers loss, the qadi must divide at the demand of any one partner. We will unpack the condition and the mechanism of qismah jabriyyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is dividing joint property. The ruling is that it becomes compulsory (wajib) on the qadi at the request of one partner.
2
Locate the condition
The condition: each partner can benefit independently from his share after division.
The passage states 'if each partner can benefit from his own share'.
3
Understand the example
Two horses: each gets a horse and can use it. No harm.
The example clarifies the condition.
4
Derive the principle
If no harm results from division, the right to seek partition is enforceable.
5
Note the absence of harm
The text explicitly says 'no harm', which is the legal cause (sabab) for compulsory division.
Harm (ḍarar) is the key preventer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۹۴۳] (۱۲) اگر شریک میں سے ہر ایک فائدہ اٹھا سکتا ہو اپنے حصے سے تو ان میں سے ایک کے طلب کرنے سے تقسیم کر دی جائے گی۔ تشریح مثلا شرکت میں دو گھوڑے ہیں تقسیم کر کے دونوں کو دینے سے ہر ایک اپنے اپنے گھوڑے سے فائدہ اٹھا سکتا ہے، ایسی صورت میں ایک شریک بھی تقسیم کا مطالبہ کرے گا تو تقسیم کر دی جائے گی۔ تقسیم کرنے سے کسی کو نقصان نہیں ہے اس لئے تقسیم کر دے۔
[2943] (12) If each partner can benefit from his own share, then at the request of one of them, division will be made. Explanation: For example, in a partnership there are two horses; by dividing and giving one to each, each can benefit from his own horse. In such a case, if one partner demands division, division will be made. There is no harm to anyone from division, so he divides.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on compulsory partition (qismah jabriyyah) when every partner can independently benefit from his share after division. The author gives the example of two horses owned jointly. If divided, each partner receives one horse and can use it for riding or work, so there is no loss (ḍarar). In such a scenario, even if only one partner requests division, the qadi must enforce it. The underlying principle is that division does not cause harm, and the right to seek separation from joint ownership is recognized. This is consistent with the Hanafi position that compulsory partition is allowed when no harm results.

Key Terms
تقسیم (taqsīm): Division of jointly owned property into separate shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When each partner can benefit from his share and no harm results
C) Only when the property cannot be divided
D) When the partners disagree

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, compulsory division is permitted only when it causes no harm to any partner.

Division When One Partner Is Harmed: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on division when one partner is harmed, with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on compulsory partition when one partner benefits and the other is harmed, distinguishing between requests by the larger and smaller shareholder.

Core Idea
Division is granted to the benefiting partner but refused to the harming partner, based on 'no harm and no reciprocating harm.'

This passage presents a detailed fiqh case where two partners share three oxen. The ruling hinges on who requests division and the consequence of benefit or harm. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the Hanafi principles in property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is compulsory partition (qismah jabariyyah) when one partner benefits and the other is harmed. The ruling: granted to the larger shareholder, denied to the smaller unless there is another benefit.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith 'La darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocating harm). It is a hadith from Ibn Abbas, narrated in Ibn Majah and Dar Qutni.
The hadith is used to justify both principles: allow benefit even if incidental harm, and prevent self-harm.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit prohibition of causing harm and reciprocating harm indicates that actions leading to harm without valid reason are not allowed. The qadi must prevent a person from harming himself.
The phrase 'la darar' prohibits initiating harm; 'la dirar' prohibits retaliating with harm.
4
Apply the two principles
Principle 1: If a person seeks his own benefit and it causes incidental harm to another, he is allowed. Principle 2: If a person wants to harm himself, the qadi must prevent it.
The larger shareholder benefits (can plough) – division granted. The smaller shareholder would be harmed (cannot plough) – division refused unless he intends to sell.
5
Note the thamarah (practical consequence)
The qadi's decision depends on who requests and the purpose. If the smaller shareholder wants to sell his share, division is allowed because that benefits him.
The ruling balances individual benefit with judicial protection from harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۹۴۴] (۱۳) اور اگر ایک فائدہ اٹھائے اور دوسرا نقصان اپنا حصہ کم ہونے کی وجہ سے، پس اگر زائد حصے والا طلب کرے تو تقسیم کر دی جائے گی۔ اور اگر کم والا طلب کرے تو تقسیم نہیں کی جائے گی۔ مثلا دو آدمیوں کے درمیان تین بیل ہیں۔ ایک کا حصہ دو گنا ہے جس کی وجہ سے دو بیل مل جائیں گے اور ہل چلا سکے گا۔ اور دوسرے کا حصہ ایک گنا ہے جس کی وجہ سے اس کو ایک بیل ملے گا اور ایک بیل سے ہل نہیں چلا سکے گا۔ جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ تقسیم ہونے کے بعد بڑا حصہ دار اپنے حصے سے فائدہ اٹھا سکے گا اور چھوٹا حصہ دار اپنے حصے سے کما حقہ فائدہ نہیں اٹھا سکے گا۔ ایسی صورت میں بڑا حصہ دار تقسیم کا مطالبہ کرے تو تقسیم کی جائے گی۔ اور چھوٹا حصہ دار تقسیم کا مطالبہ کرے تو تقسیم نہیں کی جائے گی۔ بڑا حصہ دار جب تقسیم کا مطالبہ کر رہا ہے تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ تقسیم کر دیں تاکہ میں اپنے حصے سے آزادگی کے ساتھ فائدہ اٹھا سکوں اور جب چاہوں دو بیل سے ہل چلالوں، چاہے دوسرے کو نقصان ہو جائے ۔ کیونکہ میں نے دوسرے کو ہمیشہ فائدہ دینے کی ذمہ داری نہیں لی ہے۔ اس لئے اس کے کہنے پر تقسیم کر دی جائے گی۔ اور کم حصہ دار جب مطالبہ کر رہا ہے کہ تقسیم کر دیں اور بڑا حصہ دار خاموش ہے تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ مجھے نقصان ہوتا ہے تو ہونے دو میں اپنے فائدے کے حق میں متعنت اور متسدد ہوں۔ اس لئے اس کے نقصان ملحوظ رکھتے ہوئے قاضی اس کے کہنے پر تقسیم نہیں کرے گا۔ کیونکہ قاضی کو اس لئے مقرر کیا گیا ہے کوئی اپنا نقصان کرنا چاہے تو اس کو نقصان نہ کرنے دے۔ البتہ کوئی اور فائدہ ہو مثلا اپنے حصے کو مناسب قیمت میں بیچ کر فائدہ اٹھانا چاہے تو ایسی صورت میں قاضی تقسیم کر دے۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ دو اصولوں پر متفرع ہے (۱) کوئی آدمی اپنا فائدہ ملحوظ رکھنا چاہے، اس سے دوسرے کو نقصان ہو جائے تو اس کو اجازت ہوگی بشر طیکه خواہ مخواہ دوسرے کو نقصان دینا مقصود نہ ہو (۲) اور دوسرا اصول یہ ہے کہ کوئی اپنا نقصان کرنا چاہے تو قاضی کی ذمہ داری ہے کہ اس کو نقصان سے بچائے ، اس کے لئے حدیث یہ ہے۔ عن ابن عباس قال قال رسول الله ﷺ لا ضرر ولا ضرار (الف) (ابن ماجه شریف، باب من بنی فی حقه ما یضر بجاره ص ۱۳۳۵، نمبر ۲۳۴۱ / دار قطنی ، کتاب البیوع، ج تالیچ، ص ۶۴ نمبر ۳۰۶۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ نہ کسی کو نقصان دے اور نہ نقصان اٹھائے۔
[2944] (13) And if one benefits and the other is harmed due to his small share, then if the one with the larger share requests, division will be made. And if the one with the smaller share requests, it will not be divided. For example, between two people there are three oxen. One's share is double, so he will get two oxen and can plough. The other's share is single, so he will get one ox and cannot plough with one ox. The conclusion is that after division, the larger shareholder will benefit from his share, and the smaller shareholder will not fully benefit from his share. In such a case, if the larger shareholder demands division, it will be divided. And if the smaller shareholder demands division, it will not be divided. When the larger shareholder demands division, it means: divide so that I can freely benefit from my share and plough with two oxen whenever I want, even if it harms the other. Because I have not undertaken the responsibility to always give benefit to the other. So at his request, division will be made. And when the smaller shareholder demands division while the larger is silent, it means: even if I suffer loss, let it be; I am obstinate regarding my benefit. So keeping his harm in view, the qadi will not divide at his request. Because the qadi is appointed to prevent someone from harming themselves. However, if there is some other benefit, for example he wants to benefit by selling his share at a fair price, then in that case the qadi will divide. The principle: This issue branches on two principles: (1) If a person wants to keep his benefit, and it causes harm to another, he will be allowed, provided that causing harm to the other is not his primary intention. (2) And the second principle is that if someone wants to harm himself, it is the qadi's responsibility to save him from harm. For this, there is a hadith: from Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm.' (Ibn Majah, Bab man bana fi haqqihi ma yadurru bijarihi, p. 1335, no. 2341; Dar Qutni, Kitab al-Buyu', vol. 3, p. 64, no. 3060). From this hadith, it is learned that one should neither give harm nor receive harm.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, presenting a detailed case of compulsory partition where one partner benefits and the other suffers due to the small size of his share. The author illustrates with three oxen owned by two partners: one has a two-thirds share (two oxen), the other a one-third share (one ox). After division, the first can plough with his two oxen, but the second cannot plough with one ox alone. The ruling distinguishes based on who requests division. If the larger shareholder requests, division is granted because he seeks to benefit from his share without obligation to the other. The qadi permits this as long as the primary intention is not to harm the other (the first principle: seeking one's own benefit is allowed even if incidental harm occurs). If the smaller shareholder requests, division is not granted because he would be harming himself, and the qadi must prevent self-harm (the second principle). However, if the smaller shareholder intends to sell his share and benefit from the price, then division is permitted. The proof is the hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no causing harm), which underscores the duty to avoid both inflicting and suffering harm. This is a nuanced ruling that balances individual benefit with judicial protection from harm.

Key Terms
لا ضرر ولا ضرار (lā ḍarar wa lā ḍirār): A legal maxim meaning no harm is to be inflicted and no harm is to be reciprocated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of three oxen, why is division granted when the larger shareholder requests it but not when the smaller does?
A) Because the larger shareholder has more money
B) Because the larger shareholder benefits while the smaller suffers harm
C) Because the smaller shareholder is lying
D) Because the qadi favors the rich

Key Takeaway
Division is granted to the partner who benefits, but refused to the partner who would harm himself, as per the prophetic maxim against harm.
